1. Introduction


According to the 2022 Philippine Water Supply and Sanitation Master Plan, the Government
of the Philippines (GoP) plans to achieve 100 percent safe water supply and sanitation (WSS)
coverage by 2030. As of 2020, the overall coverage of safely managed water supply is at 47
percent, while safely managed sanitation services are approximately at 61 percent.1 The
AWSPSA identifies key strategies that the GoP needs to implement to fulfill its 2030 universal
WSS coverage targets.

The GoP recognizes the need for investments and sectoral reforms to achieve the WSS
targets and has requested for World Bank financing for the proposed Accelerated Water and
Sanitation Project in Selected Areas (AWSPSA). The AWSPSA aims to increase access to
safe water supply, and sanitation (WASH) services and operational efficiency and quality of
selected water and sanitation service providers. The proposed AWSPSA is aimed to support
(a) to resolve water security and sanitation issues in poor and lagging areas in the country,
and in support of affirmative action for peace and security, (b) to provide sustainable,
affordable, and safe water supply, and adequate and safely managed sanitation in a more
efficient and more effective way, and (c) to mitigate environmental risks arising from the
depletion of groundwater, especially in small islands.

Environmental and social instruments such as Labor Management Procedures (LMP), Chance
Find Procedure (CFP), Resettlement Policy Framework (RPF), Stakeholder Engagement Plan
(SEP), and Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA), will also be drafted as part
of the environmental and social safeguards for AWSPSA implementation.

         1.1 Purpose of the Environmental and Social Management Framework (ESMF)
This ESMF will serve as a guide to the national and local implementing agencies on the
assessment of environmental and social risks and impacts of AWSPSA subprojects during
project implementation. The ESMF will outline the requirements of the project to be consistent
with the environmental and social safeguard policies of WB and national/local government
agencies.
The AWSPSA project components were subjected to project E&S screening at the beginning
of the assessment process. This screening was done in accordance with the four WB ESF
classifications: High Risk, Substantial Risk, Moderate Risk, and Low Risk. Relevant factors
are considered when determining the appropriate risk classification, including the project's
type, location, sensitivity, and scale; the nature and extent of potential environmental and
social risks and impacts; and the borrower's (in this case, the DPWH) capacity and
commitment to manage the project's environmental and social risks and impacts in a way that
complies with WB Environmental and Social Safeguards.

Based on the WB generated AWSPSA Concept Environmental and Social Review Summary
Report (CESRSR), the Project risks were assessed to be ‘Substantial’ for both the
Environment and Social aspects. Thus, in conformance with WB ESS1, an ESIA was prepared
for AWSPSA.

drinking water, sanitation and hygiene 2000‒2020: Five years into the SDGs”, page 119 and 139,

On the other hand, as mandated by the Philippine Environmental Impact Statement System
(PEISS), water supply projects, whether new or existing with modifications, are classified as
Category B Projects, or Non-Environmentally Critical Projects (Non-ECP), based on the
project screening indicated in EMB MC 2014-05 and EMB MC 2024-003. These projects are
located within Environmentally Critical Areas (ECA) and must obtain an Environmental
Compliance Certificate (ECC) or Certificate of Non-Coverage (CNC) before the
implementation of the project. The AWSPSA's Category D subprojects, which include the
Level I and Level II water supply systems, will be required to secure CNC, and their application
must be accompanied by a Project Description.

The other safeguard instruments, such as the LMP, CFP, RPF, and SEP, will be prepared
based on the results and findings from the ESIA and ESMF. These E&S instruments shall be
prepared concurrently with this ESMF in accordance with the WB ESSs. Figure 1 presents
the ESMF Schematic Diagram.

   1.2 Project Description
         1.2.1 Project Background

The Philippine water supply and sanitation (WSS) sector faces numerous systemic
challenges, including fragmented governance, poor coordination among agencies, and
underdeveloped infrastructure. Despite the country’s abundant water resources, the uneven
distribution of water and the lack of access to clean water and sanitation facilities leave many
communities vulnerable to waterborne diseases. Financially and technically under-equipped
water service providers (WSPs), coupled with widespread unsafe water sources and
inadequate sanitation, further exacerbate the issue. With the looming threat of a worsening
water crisis by 2040, substantial investment and strategic interventions are urgently needed
to expand coverage and improve the efficiency of WSS services nationwide.

In response, the Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas (AWSPSA) aims
to support the Philippine government’s efforts to achieve SDG 6 and the objectives outlined in
the Philippine Development Plan 2023–2028. The project seeks to address the sector’s
institutional fragmentation by strengthening coordination, improving policy frameworks, and
enhancing monitoring and data collection on water resources. By developing a comprehensive
inventory of water infrastructure and integrating water management across hydrologic



| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                                     3
                                                                                                                                  Official Use Only
                                                                              Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                                        Environment and Social Management Framework



boundaries rather than political jurisdictions, the AWSPSA will promote efficient resource use,
improve service delivery, and build resilience against the impacts of climate change,
urbanization, and population growth.

In the Philippines, one in every four children under five years old are shorter than the average
child in the world for their age which is attributed to poor water, sanitation and hygiene
(WASH). Increasing the poor's access to WASH—including inclusive WASH facilities in public
health and education institutions—is essential to enhancing public health, closing gender
disparities, and fostering more equitable economic growth.

The AWSPSA is fully aligned with the Nationally Determined Contributions 2021, National
Climate Change Action Plan 2011-2028, National Adaptation Plan, and Philippine
Development Plan 2023-2028. In terms of risks related to climate mitigation, the operation is
not expected to have a significant negative impact on the country’s low-GreenHouse Gas
(GHG)-emissions development pathways. Climate hazards such as flooding, and drought may
pose a risk to the operation and the achievement of the Project Development Objective (PDO).
While these risks could potentially affect the result of increased access to sustainable and safe
WASH services, the design will consider climate-resilient measures to reduce the risks from
climate hazards to an acceptable level.2

         1.2.2 Project Objectives

The Project aims to increase access to safely managed water supply and sanitation services
and improve operational performance of water service providers in project areas.

The AWSPSA will contribute to improve efficiency of infrastructure services in selected areas
through the rehabilitation, upgrading, expansion, construction and more efficient WSS
systems; and increase resilience to natural disasters and climate change-exacerbated risks,
by developing new sustainable water sources and ceasing the use of depleted groundwater
sources. The AWSPSA will also contribute to the cross-cutting theme on governance, scaling
up development impact across the Philippine archipelago by strengthening implementation
capacity, particularly at the local level. It will strengthen the implementation capacity of
selected LGUs as well as vertical and horizontal coordination across the NG and LGUs.

         1.2.3 Project Components




Component 1. Infrastructure Investments for Safe, Climate Resilient Water Supply
Services (US$213.30 million: US$197.55 million IBRD, US$11.32 GoP counterpart,


2
 Source: World Bank Group, https://climatesmartwater.org/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2021/03/Resilient-Water-Infrastructure-
Design-Brief.pdf, last accessed on 13 August 2024.




| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                                 4
                                                                                                                              Official Use Only
                                                                  Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                            Environment and Social Management Framework



US$4.43 million private capital). This component aims to increase access to safely managed
water supply services through infrastructure projects that will include construction and/or
rehabilitation of complete water supply systems, from source development to household
connections.

Subcomponent 1a. Water Supply Infrastructure Investments in the Loboc Cluster,
Bohol Province (US$148.63 million, including US$4.21 million private capital).This
subcomponent will support: (i) the development of a bulk water supply system sourced from
the Loboc River with a capacity of 60,000 cubic meters/day; and (ii) the rehabilitation,
upgrading, and expansion of existing water distribution systems of LGU-run WSPs. The
development of the bulk water system will include the construction of: (i) an intake facility; (ii)
a water treatment plant (WTP); (iii) reservoirs for treated water; (iv) approximately 35
kilometers of transmission lines; (v) more than 170 kilometers of offtake pipelines; (vi) six
booster stations; and the installation of (vii) flow meters and pressure reducing valves; and
(viii) eight offtake points for the eight beneficiary LGUs.

Subcomponent 1b. Water supply infrastructure investments in Siargao Island in
Surigao del Norte Province (US$45.23 million). This subcomponent aims to provide safe
and reliable water supply for Siargao Island residents and tourists by developing and
enhancing the island’s water infrastructure. It will support eight municipalities that are
characterized as rural and classified as 4th to 5th class – Dapa, General Luna, Del Carmen,
Pilar, Santa Monica, San Isidro, San Benito, and Socorro. This activity will involve: (i) the
construction of new water source projects (new deep well sources with reservoirs); and (ii) the
upgrading, rehabilitation, and/or expansion of the water distribution systems of the 8 LGUs.

Subcomponent 1c. Water supply infrastructure investments in Jolo and neighboring
barangays in Indanan and Patikul (US$21.72 million).This subcomponent will support the
following activities: (i) upgrading of 3 spring water sources by replacing pumps, installing
climate resilient housing, and providing treatment facilities to treat water to drinking water
standards; and (ii) rehabilitation/replacement/expansion of the water distribution systems.

Component 2. Infrastructure Investments for Safe and Climate Resilient Sanitation and
WASH Services in Institutions (US$12.90 million, of which US$12.32 million IBRD). This
subcomponent will support various activities that will expand safe sanitation and WASH
services and promote WASH practices at the municipality, school, and household levels.

Subcomponent 2a. Sanitation and WASH in Bohol (US$3.70 million). The sanitation
infrastructure under this subcomponent will include improvement or construction of new
WASH facilities for 54 public schools, 136 daycare centers, and 62 health centers that will
benefit around 45,225 people.

Subcomponent 2b. Sanitation and WASH in Siargao, Surigao del Norte (US$5.58
million).The sanitation project will include the construction of a septage treatment plant
(SpTP). While there is an existing SpTP in the municipality of Del Carmen, it is only used
sparingly and its capacity for treatment is not sufficient. The proposed SpTP will complete the
capacity requirements for FSM in the island.




| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                     5
                                                                                                                  Official Use Only
                                                                     Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                               Environment and Social Management Framework



Subcomponent 2c. Sanitation and WASH in Jolo, Sulu (US$3.62 million).The project will
include the improvement or construction of new WASH facilities for public schools, daycare
centers, and social welfare facilities.

Component 3. Institutional Reforms and Capacity Building (US$13.44 million,
IBRD).This component will support fundamental institutional reforms and capacity-building of
provinces, LGUs, and WSPs. It aims to improve the efficiency of service provision and
sustainability of WSS services. The institutional reforms will ensure that an adequate
operational unit will be established and/or contracted for the sustainable O&M of WSS facilities
and services.

Component 4. Project Management and Technical Assistance (US$28.58 million IBRD).
This component will include: (i) project management, supervision and operating costs of the
DPWH and the DILG Project Management Offices; and (ii) technical assistance (TA) and
consulting services for the design of sub-projects and related studies; support to newly created
water sector institutions; and preparation of engineering, social, and environmental technical
studies for other prioritized provinces that will form a pipeline for subsequent WSS
investments.

Subcomponent 4a Project Management for Infrastructure (US$15.05 million). This
subcomponent will support project management for the DPWH and the DILG. Eligible activities
include consulting and non-consulting services, training, and relevant operating costs required
to implement the AWSPSA in accordance with GoP and World Bank policy and guidelines.

Subcomponent 4b. Technical Assistance (US$13.53 million).This subcomponent will
include LGU-level activities.

                                                    Component         5.    Contingent        Emergency
                                                    Response Component (US$0 million). This zero
                                                    cost component will allow the GoP to quickly
                                                    access project funds in response to an eligible
                                                    disaster, emergency, or crisis. It will allow rapid re-
                                                    allocation of uncommitted project funds to address
                                                    urgent needs in the event of a natural disaster or
                                                    crisis     (including      public       health-related
                                                    emergencies). A declaration of a state of calamity
                                                    by a qualified national or subnational authority, in
                                                    accordance with applicable laws, could serve as a
                                                    possible trigger to activate this component.



                                                          1.2.4 Project Implementation
                                                    Arrangements

                                           The main Implementing Agency for this project is
                                           the Department of Public Works and Highways
                                           (DPWH), which is the executive branch of the GoP
that is exclusively charged with the responsibility of serving as “the State's engineering and




| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                        6
                                                                                                                     Official Use Only
                                                                    Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                              Environment and Social Management Framework



construction arm”. The Project Management Office (PMO) will be managed by the DPWH's
Unified Project Management Office (UPMO) - Flood Control Management Cluster (FCMC).
The day-to-day project management will fall within the purview of the UPMO-FCMC. The
DPWH's corresponding procurement and financial management-related units will handle the
fiduciary function, while the UPMO-FCMC will handle contracts and prepare all necessary
procurement documents. In addition, UPMO-FCMC will assist with the initial processing and
review of contractor billings, as well as the preparation and submission of Statements of
Expenditures and Withdrawal Applications to the WB. UPMO-FCMC will monitor that the
permits required for the AWSPSA sub-projects are obtained, oversee the application of E&S
mitigation measures by the AWSPSA subprojects, and submit periodic reports to the WB
regarding contractor compliances with ESMP commitments.

For the concerned LGUs, they are required to conduct consultations in collaboration with the
AWSPSA/UPMO-FCMC during the planning and execution of the E&S subproject plans. They
will review and approve the permits relevant to the AWSPSA subprojects as well as provide
the RDC the required endorsements. When required, the involved Local Government Units
(LGUs) will also take part in the monitoring of the contractor's adherence to ECC/EMP/EMoP
requirements and activities.

                                                    1.3 Project Location
                                                    The Project will be implemented initially (see
                                                    Appendix 1 for the prioritization criteria) in three
                                                    provinces located in the regions of Visayas (Bohol
                                                    Province) and Mindanao (Provinces of Surigao
                                                    del Norte and Sulu).

                                                    Proposed project provinces have several natural
                                                    features with ecological importance. For example,
                                                    Bohol is renowned for its coral reefs and unique
                                                    geological formations, such as the Chocolate Hills
                                                    in Carmen, featuring over 1,200 symmetrical
                                                    mounds that turn cocoa-brown in the dry season.
                                                    The province is also home to the Tarsier sanctuary,
                                                    a large forest area dedicated to protecting the
                                                    endangered tarsiers, small nocturnal primates;
                                                    Siargao: known as the surfing capital of the
                                                    Philippines, it boasts long sandy beaches, natural
                                                    caves, and impressive rock formations; and Sulu
                                                    features diverse ecosystems, including tropical
                                                    forests, coastal areas, and marine habitats, with
                                                    multiple protected areas.

In the pilot areas of Bohol and Siargao, there are no significant impacts on Indigenous Peoples
(IPs) groups, nor are there any Ancestral Domain Claims in these provinces.

                                                                              Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                                        Environment and Social Management Framework



                1.4 Work Program
       The pre-construction, construction, operations, and decommissioning phases of the AWSPSA
       will be covered by an environmental and social risk assessment based on the DPWH SEMS
       and the World Bank ESF. This allows for the correction or address of any additional or new
       impacts that may arise at any point during the project's phases. The Project Contractor shall
       carry out all water supply and sanitation operations as listed in the table below, under the
       direction and supervision of DPWH and/or its construction consultant and supervisor (as
       required by WB).
       Table 1. Work Program for water supply and sanitation

                1.5 Structure of ESMF

       The Environmental and Social Management Framework (ESMF) for the Accelerated Water
       and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas (AWSPSA) commences with a comprehensive
       Project Description, detailing the project’s development objectives, key components,
       geographic scope, and implementation arrangements. It further delineates the Policy, Legal,
       and Regulatory Framework, encompassing the relevant environmental and social legislation
       of the Republic of the Philippines, as well as the applicable World Bank Environmental and



       | Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                          8
                                                                                                                              Official Use Only
                                                                         Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                                   Environment and Social Management Framework



Social Standards (ESS). A critical component of this section is the Gap Analysis, which
systematically identifies and analyzes discrepancies between the World Bank’s safeguard
requirements and the national regulatory framework, ensuring alignment with international
best practices and the adoption of measures to bridge identified gaps.

The ESMF also includes the Detailed guidance on the implementation of environmental and
social safeguards within the project, per project phase, including screening, preparation of
safeguard instruments, disclosure and public consultation, and grievance redress

2. Policy, Legal, and Regulatory Requirements


    2.1 Philippine Legal and Policy Framework and World Bank Environment Social
    Framework
The development of this ESMF complies with the laws, policies, regulations, and standards
of the Philippine government that place limitations on project activities to avoid, prevent,
minimize, or mitigate potential impacts on the environment and people. In addition, the
project will adhere to the Environmental and Social Framework (ESF) of the World Bank,
which outlines particular Environmental and Social Standards (ESSs) intended to prevent,
minimize, lessen, or mitigate adverse environmental and social risks and impacts of the
project.

Foreign funded projects shall be governed by Philippine environmental and social laws and
regulations as well as the safeguards policies of the International Funding Agency (the WB,
in this case), as committed in the DPWH SEMS Manual and the DPWH Department Order
159 series of 2022. The Implementing Office (UPMO-FCMC) shall level off with the Foreign
Institution to come up with an agreement in resolving any policy gaps.

         2.2 Environmental Permitting Process and Timeline

A detailed permitting flowchart has been developed to clarify the sequence and indicative duration of
required environmental approvals. This aims to streamline implementation and enhance coordination
with DENR-EMB, PAMB, and LGUs. Early engagement is strongly encouraged to prevent delays,
especially in protected or sensitive areas.

The environmental permitting phase typically takes around 2.5 to 3.5 months, assuming early
coordination with DENR and PAMB. Steps such as site screening and agency consultations should




| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                            11
                                                                                                                         Official Use Only
                                                                      Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                                Environment and Social Management Framework



begin during the Feasibility or Pre-Detailed Design stage. Delays often arise from permits like Tree
Cutting and PAMB Clearances, so early action is key.

Refer to Figure 2 below for the full flowchart.


Figure 2. Environmental Permitting Flowchart




                                                  The environmental permitting process begins with
                                                  subproject identification and preliminary site screening by
                                                  DPWH, DILG, and consultants, typically taking around 2 to
                                                  3 weeks.

                                                  This is followed by coordination with DENR-EMB, PAMB,
                                                  and LGUs, led by UPMO-FCMC and consultants, which
                                                  may take an additional 1 to 2 weeks.

                                                  Preparation of the required environmental documents
                                                  such as the IEE, ESIA, or Project Description is carried out
                                                  by consultants over a period of 3 to 4 weeks.

                                                  Once completed, the proponent submits these documents
                                                  to the EMB Regional Office. The DENR-EMB then
                                                  conducts its screening and review, which may take
                                                  between 30 to 60 calendar days, culminating in the
                                                  issuance of the ECC or CNC.

                                                  Concurrently, contractors may need to apply for
                                                  supplementary permits, including Tree Cutting Permits,
                                                  Waste Management Plans, or PAMB Clearances, which
                                                  can take 15 to 30 days.

                                                  Upon completion of permitting, the UPMO-FCMC and
                                                  contractors integrate environmental compliance measures
                                                  into the bid documents and proceed with mobilization,
                                                  usually within 1 to 2 weeks.

                                                  During project implementation, contractors and UPMO-
                                                  FCMC are responsible for the regular submission of SMRs
                                                  and CMRs on a quarterly or semi-annual basis to ensure
                                                  ongoing compliance.




Since Siargao is designated as a protected area under the National Integrated Protected Areas System
(NIPAS), securing a clearance from the Protected Area Management Board (PAMB) was identified as
a key regulatory requirement prior to proceeding with further project development activities.

Accordingly, the PAMB Clearance was obtained during the Pre-Detailed Design Stage to ensure
compliance with environmental and protected area regulations. The application, review, and approval
process took approximately two months from the date of submission to the issuance of the clearance.

         2.3 World Bank Environmental and Social Framework
In addition to national requirements, the project will also adhere to the World Bank’s Environmental and
Social Framework (ESF). The ESF outlines ten Environmental and Social Standards (ESSs) that
promote sustainable development by systematically identifying and managing environmental and social
risks. These standards cover areas such as labor and working conditions, resource efficiency,
community health and safety, land acquisition, biodiversity conservation, and stakeholder engagement.

Where there are policy differences or gaps between Philippine regulations and World Bank
requirements, the Implementing Office (UPMO-FCMC) will coordinate with the World Bank to harmonize
approaches and ensure consistent application of safeguards throughout the project lifecycle.

Table 3 presents a brief summary of the Work Bank Environmental and Social Framework

         2.4 Gap Analysis Between Applicable World Bank Environmental and Social
         Framework and GoP Regulations

Environmental and social management strategies have been developed through the World
Bank's ESF and 10 ESSs. This is done to make sure that development initiatives supported
by the Bank do not negatively impact the social and environmental conditions of the people
and environment in which they are implemented or supported. The goal of the World Bank's
assistance with the ESF-related matter is to strengthen the DPWH's capabilities in a way that
adheres to global best practices for technical, environmental, social, and health and safety
standards. For the AWSPSA, the ESF regulations will take precedence.



Borrower Framework and World Bank ESS

The Project will apply a hybrid approach:

    •    The Project will rely on the Borrower’s existing environmental and social management
         systems for aspects that are aligned with the requirements of the Philippine EIS
         System and relevant national legislation (e.g., environmental assessment, permitting,
         and compliance monitoring obligations under the DENR).

    •    For areas where gaps exist between the Borrower Framework and the WB ESF
         requirements, the Project will apply the World Bank ESS directly. This includes, but is
         not limited to, stakeholder engagement, labor and working conditions (including OHS),
         land acquisition and resettlement impacts, biodiversity risk management, Indigenous
         Peoples engagement and consent processes where applicable, and cultural heritage
         protection.


Table 4 presents the comparative/gap analysis between the WB ESSs and the GoP laws and
regulations and the corresponding measures aimed to close the gap.

3. Environmental and Social Baseline
            3.1 Overview of the Philippines’ Environmental and Social Baseline
            3.1.1 Climate and Rainfall

The Philippines has a tropical monsoonal climate with an average temperature of 27°C and
humidity levels above 70% year-round. Its climate is classified into four types based on rainfall
patterns:

      ●     Type I: Two distinct seasons; dry from November to April, wet the rest of the year.
      ●     Type II: No dry season; maximum rainfall from November to April.
      ●     Type III: Less pronounced seasons; relatively dry from November to April.
      ●     Type IV: Rainfall evenly distributed throughout the year.

Annual rainfall varies from 1,000 to 4,000 mm, averaging 2,400 mm, with 1,000 to 2,000 mm
contributing to runoff across various river basins and lakes. The country also experiences
climate variability through El Niño and La Niña events, with El Niño lasting 8 to 12 months
occurring every 2 to 7 years, while La Niña lasts 1 to 3 years and occurs every 3 to 4 years.
The environmental and social baseline of the initial projects identified are attached Appendix
1.1 for Bohol, Appendix 1.2 for Surigao del Norte, and Appendix 1.3 for Sulu.

Climate Change

Climate change projections for the Philippines indicate increasing variability in rainfall and
rising sea levels. According to the Philippine Atmospheric, Geophysical and Astronomical
Services Administration (PAGASA) and the IPCC Sixth Assessment Report, rainfall patterns
are expected to become more erratic, characterized by more frequent heavy downpours and
longer dry spells. This variability heightens the risk of both flooding and drought across the
country. At the same time, the Philippines is projected to experience sea-level rise at or above
the global average, estimated at about 0.2–0.3 meters by 2050 and up to 0.6–1.0 meters by
2100, which will intensify risks of coastal flooding, storm surge, erosion, and saltwater intrusion
(IPCC, 2021; DENR–CCC, 2023 National Adaptation Plan).

         3.1.2 Total Water Resources Potential

The country has a total water resource potential of 145,990 million cubic meters (MCM) per
year. This includes a surface water potential of 125,790 MCM per year (at 80% dependability)
and a groundwater potential of 20,200 MCM per year.

Region XIII has the highest water resource potential among all administrative regions,
accounting for 12.5% (approximately 18,000 MCM per year) of the country’s total. In contrast,
the National Capital Region (NCR), being the most urbanized area, has the lowest potential,
contributing less than 0.5% of the total (98 MCM per year). The figure below shows the water
resources potential of each administrative region.


Figure 3. Total Water Resources Potential per Administrative Region




Source: Philippine Water Supply and Sanitation Master Plan



         3.1.3 Water Supply

The Philippine Statistics Authority (PSA) focuses on two primary key performance indicators
(KPIs) related to water access: the access to safe water supply categorized by source and the
access to drinking water.
Access by Source. The Family Income and Expenditure Survey (FIES) conducted by the
PSA in 2015 revealed that just 87.7 percent of the Philippine population had access to water
sources deemed safe. The remaining 12.3 percent relied on unsafe water sources, such as
dug wells, lakes, water sold by vendors, rainwater, and unprotected springs, rivers, and
streams.
Access to Drinking Water. Access to safe and affordable drinking water is a key indicator
highlighted in the Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs). Currently, the country lacks a
system for collecting nationwide data on access to safe drinking water. The closest available
data comes from the PSA's National Demographic and Health Survey (NDHS). The 2013
NDHS categorizes drinking water sources as improved or unimproved; however, this
classification does not assess water quality, as water from improved sources can still become
contaminated during transport and storage.




| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                         23

                                                                                                                      Official Use Only
                                                                  Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                            Environment and Social Management Framework



         3.1.3.1 Type of Service Providers

The National Water Resources Board (NWRB) established a national water survey for Water
Service Providers (WSPs) called Listahang Tubig. This survey offers information on service
levels based on management type. As of December 2018, the database has recorded a total
of 24,821 registered WSPs.
         3.1.3.2 Level of Service

Water supply in the Philippines is primarily provided by WSPs, but many households rely on
private sources like deep wells and rainwater collectors. The National Economic and
Development Authority (NEDA) defines three service levels for water supply:

    ●    Level I: Point sources such as protected wells or developed springs without a
         distribution system. Users must fetch water from the source, typically serving about 15
         households within 250 meters. This is common in rural areas.
    ●    Level II: Communal faucet systems that include a piped network and serve four to six
         households within 25 meters of a communal faucet. This system is suitable for densely
         clustered rural and urban fringe areas.
    ●    Level III: Waterworks systems featuring a source, reservoir, and individual household
         taps, designed for densely populated urban areas where households can afford direct
         connections.

Overall, about 43.6% of the population receives water from Level III systems, 11.2% from
Level II, and 45.2% from Level I systems.


         3.1.4 Sanitation

Sanitation services are characterized by three key attributes:

    ●    Facility: refers to the structure or infrastructure providing sanitation services
    ●    Usage: indicates the accessibility of the facility for households.
    ●    Management: involves the proper disposal of excreta, whether on-site or transported
         for treatment at an off-site facility.

         3.1.4.1 Septage and Sewerage Services

Households and communities are tasked with building toilets and septic tanks according to the
National Building Code (NBC). For these systems to be deemed safely managed, wastewater
and septage must be treated and disposed of properly. Maintenance of on-site systems is the
responsibility of service providers, which offer sanitation services such as desludging and
sewerage. These services are often grouped under wastewater or fecal sludge management
and encompass the collection, treatment, and disposal of waste.

Outside Mega Manila, sanitation service providers mainly include water districts (WDs), local
government unit (LGU)-operated utilities, and private companies, with some known by names
like Malabanan and Pozo Negro. However, many private providers lack treatment facilities and
may dispose of waste improperly. New compliant private entities are emerging, adhering to
regulations from the Department of Health (DOH) and the Environmental Management Bureau
(EMB). Registered providers must report their activities to local governments and can only
operate in approved areas.




| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                     24

                                                                                                                  Official Use Only
                                                                  Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                            Environment and Social Management Framework



Currently, there is no comprehensive survey of sanitation service providers outside Mega
Manila, but unofficial estimates suggest around 36 septage treatment facilities exist across the
country, primarily managed by WDs, with some services offered by LGUs. In some remote
areas, open defecation is still practiced due to the absence of toilets or proper sanitation
facilities. This practice increases the risk of disease transmission and is a major public health
issue. Access to improved sanitation is critical for preventing the spread of waterborne
diseases. While urban areas generally have access to modern sanitation systems such as
septic tanks and sewerage, rural areas face significant gaps. Many rural communities still rely
on traditional pit latrines, which can easily contaminate nearby water sources, particularly in
densely populated areas.

         3.1.5 Biodiversity

The Philippines is one of the world’s megadiverse countries and a recognized biodiversity
hotspot, home to over 52,000 species—more than half of which are endemic. Its diverse
ecosystems include tropical forests, freshwater bodies, wetlands, mangroves, coral reefs, and
seagrass beds, all of which play critical roles in regulating water supply, maintaining watershed
functions, and supporting ecological balance.
The country’s biodiversity faces significant threats from deforestation, land conversion, water
pollution, over extraction of water resources, and climate change. These pressures can
degrade habitats and reduce ecosystem services that are vital to water resource sustainability.
This project must therefore be carefully planned to avoid impacts on ecologically sensitive
areas such as forests, riparian zones, and protected areas. Biodiversity assessments should
be conducted as part of project preparation, and mitigation measures—including buffer zones,
erosion control, habitat protection, and community engagement—should be integrated into
project design in line with national environmental laws such as the Wildlife Act (RA 9147) and
the NIPAS Act (RA 11038).


         3.1.6 Community Health and Water Supply


The availability of healthcare services is uneven across the Philippines, with rural areas
experiencing limited access to health facilities. This exacerbates the health risks associated
with poor water quality, as communities may lack adequate treatment and prevention options
for waterborne diseases particularly in rural and underserved urban areas, waterborne
diseases like diarrhea, cholera, and dysentery are major health concerns. The lack of access
to safe and clean drinking water is a primary cause of these diseases, which affect vulnerable
populations, especially children under five years of age.

Nearly 90% of households in cities like Metro Manila are connected to a reliable water supply.
However, In rural areas, water is sourced mainly from groundwater (wells) and surface water
(rivers, lakes, springs). These sources are often not treated or are poorly managed, leading to
contamination and health risks. Even in areas where there is some level of infrastructure, it is
often insufficient, with many communities lacking piped water systems or facing unreliable
water supply.

         3.1.7 Safety and Security

Construction may disrupt local roads and public spaces, increase traffic hazards and limit
community access to essential services. Clear communication and traffic management are


| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                     25

                                                                                                                  Official Use Only
                                                                  Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                            Environment and Social Management Framework



crucial to ensure safety. Projects in areas prone to natural hazards like floods, earthquakes,
or landslides must also be carefully planned to ensure infrastructure resilience and avoid
exacerbating safety risks.

Land acquisition and resource conflicts are common security concerns. Disputes over land
rights, especially in rural areas with unclear land tenure, can arise, potentially leading to
displacement. Proper consultations and transparent compensation processes are essential to
avoid conflicts.

Water infrastructure such as pipelines, pumping stations, and treatment plants are at risk of
theft and vandalism, particularly in communities that feel excluded from the project. To reduce
these risks, infrastructure should be protected with security systems, monitoring, and
community involvement. In high-risk areas, security personnel may be necessary to safeguard
construction sites and infrastructure. Coordination with local law enforcement and community
leaders is key to preventing tensions or confrontations.

         3.1.8 Socio Economic Profile and Land Tenure


The Philippines has a population of approximately 113 million people (as of 2025), with
significant urbanization, a growing economy, with GDP around $400 billion USD. However,
rural areas, including remote communities, still make up a large proportion of the population
and poverty remains a challenge, particularly in rural areas, where access to water
infrastructure is limited. Urban areas experience high population density, while rural areas may
have fewer residents but face issues such as limited access to water and sanitation.

Private land ownership is prevalent in urban and agricultural areas. For a water supply project,
land acquisition from private owners may be necessary, especially for the construction of
pipelines, treatment plants, or reservoirs. And when the project requires private land, the
DPWH follows legal procedures for land acquisition, including fair compensation. In cases of
involuntary resettlement, the Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) ensures that displaced
individuals receive support, including relocation and livelihood assistance.

Ensuring that all affected communities are consulted during the planning and implementation
of the water supply project is crucial. Public consultations are required by law, and affected
communities, especially vulnerable groups, should be included in the decision-making
process.

         3.1.9 Cultural Heritage

The National Cultural Heritage Act of 2009 (Republic Act No. 10066) and the National
Integrated Protected Areas System Act (Republic Act No. 7586) provide protection to
significant cultural, historical, and natural heritage in the Philippines. These laws require that
any infrastructure projects, including water supply initiatives, be evaluated for potential impacts
on cultural resources.
The Department of National Defense and National Historical Commission of the Philippines
(NHCP) is responsible for safeguarding cultural properties and historical sites, which may
require special permits for construction in areas with known cultural or historical significance.
A thorough Cultural Heritage Impact Assessment (CHIA) should be conducted to identify and
document any significant cultural, historical, or archaeological sites within the project area.



| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                     26

                                                                                                                  Official Use Only
                                                                  Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                            Environment and Social Management Framework



This assessment will guide the project in understanding the potential impacts and the
necessary steps to mitigate them.


         3.2 Subproject-specific Environmental Baseline
         3.2.1 Environmental Baseline for Bohol

Bohol experiences a tropical climate with a distinct dry season from November to April and a
wet season from May to October. Annual rainfall averages around 1,500 to 2,000 mm, with
variability depending on topography. The province has a relatively high-water resources
potential, with numerous rivers and streams, including the major Loboc, Abatan, and Inabanga
rivers, and widespread groundwater availability due to its karst topography. Water supply is
generally adequate in urban centers but can be limited in rural and upland areas, especially
during the dry months. Groundwater serves as the main source for domestic and agricultural
use, though there are concerns about over-extraction and saltwater intrusion in coastal areas.
Sanitation coverage in Bohol has improved, particularly in city centers, but rural communities
still face challenges such as reliance on pit latrines and inadequate wastewater treatment.
Bohol boasts rich biodiversity, both terrestrial and marine, including the endangered Philippine
tarsier, various species of bats and birds, coral reefs, and seagrass beds. However, pressures
such as habitat loss, deforestation, and poorly managed tourism threaten its ecological
integrity.
         3.2.2 Environmental Baseline for Siargao

Siargao, part of Surigao del Norte, has a Type II climate characterized by the absence of a
dry season and heavy rainfall concentrated from November to February, with annual
precipitation ranging from 2,000 to 3,000 mm. Due to its island geography and limited land
area, Siargao’s total water resources potential is moderate. It relies primarily on small surface
springs, minor rivers, and groundwater from shallow aquifers, many of which are vulnerable to
seasonal fluctuations and saltwater intrusion. Water supply systems vary significantly across
the island; some barangays benefit from piped water systems, while others depend on
communal wells and rainwater collection. During peak tourism seasons or prolonged dry
periods, supply shortages have been observed. Sanitation remains a concern, particularly in
informal settlements and tourism hotspots, where wastewater and septage management are
limited, increasing the risk of coastal water pollution. Siargao’s biodiversity is concentrated in
its coastal ecosystems, with extensive mangrove forests, coral reefs, and seagrass beds
supporting a variety of marine life. The rapid growth of tourism poses challenges related to
habitat degradation, coral reef damage, and increased solid waste generation.
         3.2.3 Environmental Baseline for Jolo

Jolo, located in the Sulu Archipelago, has a Type IV climate with rainfall fairly evenly
distributed throughout the year, typically averaging around 2,500 mm annually. The island's
water resources potential is constrained by its limited freshwater catchments, small watershed
areas, and relatively shallow aquifers. Water supply is a persistent issue; many households
rely on communal deep wells, rainwater harvesting, and small-scale systems. Seasonal
scarcity and water quality concerns, such as contamination from surface runoff and poor
sanitation, affect supply reliability. Sanitation infrastructure in Jolo is underdeveloped. Many
communities lack access to improved toilets and septage treatment facilities, leading to open
defecation and direct discharge of waste into water bodies. These issues are compounded by
dense settlement areas and limited waste management services. Despite these challenges,
Jolo harbors unique biodiversity, including native forest species and rich coastal ecosystems.


| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                     27

                                                                                                                  Official Use Only
                                                                  Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                            Environment and Social Management Framework



However, biodiversity is under increasing threat from illegal logging, land conversion,
overfishing, and limited environmental governance.




         3.3 Subproject-specific Social Baseline

         3.3.1 Bohol

The Bohol Water Supply Project (BWSP) covers eight Local Government Units (LGUs),
spanning a total of 111 barangays, with a population of 264,817 based on the 2020 Census,
which represents 18.99% of Bohol’s total population. These LGUs occupy 318.14 km², or
7.73% of the province's total land area, with a population density of 832 persons per square
kilometer, significantly higher than the provincial average of 339/km². The most populous
areas within the project are Tagbilaran City, Dauis, and Panglao, while the least populated are
Sikatuna and Corella. The urban population is growing steadily, particularly in tourism-driven
areas such as Dauis and Panglao, with Tagbilaran City exhibiting the highest population
density.

Indigenous Peoples (IPs) in Bohol include the Visayan Eskaya, Ati, and Badjao. The Badjao
community, located in Barangay Totolan, Dauis, consists of 1,397 people. While there are
listed indigenous cultural communities in municipalities across Bohol, only one of the
municipalities included in the project has a community of Indigenous People, and that is the
Municipality of Dauis, which serves as home to 120 Badjao households.

The majority of the sites recommended for the BWSP scheme are uninhabited government-
owned properties, with a few uninhabited private properties also identified. Importantly, no
residents or Indigenous communities are present in the sites designated for the major project
facilities.

         3.3.2 Siargao in Surigao Del Norte

Siargao’s population has steadily increased over the years, with varying growth rates across
municipalities. Based on the 2020 Census of Population and Housing (PSA), the total
population reached 136,092, showing a growth rate of 3.44% from 2015 to 2020. General
Luna experienced the highest growth rate at 6.73%, while San Benito had the lowest at 0.99%.
The number of households in Siargao was 34,996 in 2020, with Dapa and Socorro having the
highest household counts of 6,591 and 5,849, respectively.

Regarding displacement, the proposed water supply sites across the eight municipalities are
situated on government-owned lands or schools, posing no risk of involuntary resettlement
(IR). For example, the Dapa site is within Sta. Fe Elementary School, and the Pilar site is
located at Mabuhay Elementary School. In general, the evaluation of potential IR is "Highly
Unlikely" for all proposed sites.

Regarding in-migration, the project may induce temporary migration of workers seeking
employment opportunities during construction. To mitigate this, local employment will be
prioritized, and an inventory of local labor skills will be created in collaboration with local
government units.

There are no Indigenous Peoples (IPs) in the area, and no significant cultural or lifestyle
changes are expected. Additionally, no cultural or historical resources have been identified in


| Environmental and Social Management Framework | November 2025                                                     28

                                                                                                                  Official Use Only
                                                                  Accelerated Water and Sanitation Project in Selected Areas
                                                                            Environment and Social Management Framework



the project area. If any artifacts or significant finds are discovered during excavation, work will
stop immediately, and the authorities, including the National Museum, will be notified.

         3.3.3 Jolo in Sulu

The population of the Municipality of Jolo is 137,266, according to the 2020 census, with Bus-
Bus and Asturias being the most populous barangays. These areas are likely central to
economic activity, local governance, and infrastructure development. The municipality’s
population has grown at an annual rate of 1.89%, with the most notable growth seen in
barangays such as Asturias, which had a growth rate of 3.35%.

In the Municipality of Indanan, the total population is 93,168, with a significant growth rate of
3.02%. Kajatian, which is served by the JMWD, saw a population increase of 16.36% between
2015 and 2020, indicating rapid development and an increased demand for basic services,
including water supply.

Patikul has a population of 79,564, with the barangays of Anuling, Bakong, and others showing
varying growth rates. The municipality experienced a population growth rate of 5.29% from
2015 to 2020. The Sulu Mainland Water District currently serves four barangays in Patikul,
with Gandasuli and Latih experiencing moderate to rapid population growth.

In terms of displacement, the project is located on acquired private lands, with no informal
settlers expected to be displaced. In-migration due to employment opportunities may occur,
especially during construction, but efforts will be made to prioritize local employment and
minimize migration. The project aims to support local labor and procurement of materials,
boosting the local economy.

There are no Indigenous Peoples (IPs) residing within or near the project area, so cultural or
lifestyle changes related to IPs are not anticipated.

The project area is free from known cultural resources of archaeological, historical, or cultural
significance. However, in the event of a discovery during excavation, all activities will cease,
and the proper authorities will be notified.

4. Potential Environment and Social Risks and Mitigation


The World Bank ESF states that the Bank performs environmental and social due diligence
under ESS 1 for proposed projects that fall into one of four categories: High Risk, Substantial
Risk, Moderate Risk, or Low Risk, depending on the project's type, location, sensitivity, scale,
potential environmental impacts, nature, and magnitude, as well as the borrower's
commitment and ability. Both the environmental and social aspects of the project risks were
rated as "Substantial" in the AWSPSA Concept Environmental and Social Review Summary
Report.

The project involves the development of water sources, construction and upgrading of water
transmission and distribution systems, as well as sanitation facilities such as septage
treatment and WASH installations in schools and public areas. While these investments—like
intake wells, transmission mains, and treatment facilities—offer long-term benefits, the
construction and operational phases present notable environmental risks. These include
impacts on natural habitats, groundwater over-extraction, water source contamination,
potential forest diversion, and pollution from construction debris. Occupational and community
health and safety risks are also noted, particularly during network construction. Though DPWH
has the capacity to manage these impacts, areas near protected or sensitive ecosystems
could face significant risks, thus the environmental risk is assessed as ‘Substantial’.

Socially, the project is expected to positively affect poor and vulnerable groups, including
Indigenous People as beneficiaries, by improving access to clean and affordable water and
sanitation, particularly benefiting women and girls. No adverse impacts on Indigenous Peoples
are anticipated, and they are not expected to be affected by land acquisition or civil works.
However, it also carries social risks such as land acquisition issues, potential disruption from
civil works, and concerns over equitable water resource access in non-IP areas. Health and
safety concerns arise from construction activities, with risks of pollution and traffic incidents.
Although labor influx is minimal and largely local, occupational hazards remain, especially in
extreme weather conditions. The project emphasizes the importance of inclusive design,
ensuring that WASH facilities are climate-resilient and accessible to marginalized groups,
including persons with limited mobility.

Although AWSPSA will not take place within the Indigenous People area, thus potential
adverse impacts on Indigenous People are unlikely, however risk of inequality of access to
project’s benefits is relevant. Capacity building activities will need to ensure that women,
Indigenous People and other vulnerable groups are provided spaces and opportunities to
participate and lead. Considering this, the social risk is rated as ‘Substantial’.

While the environmental and social impacts are linked to project activities such as developing
water sources, upgrading and constructing water transmission and distribution systems, and
sanitation facilities like septage treatment, these are manageable with proper mitigation
measures. The project promotes inclusive, climate-resilient infrastructure design to ensure
access for marginalized groups and persons with limited mobility will also support local
economic growth, especially in the tourism sector, and generate employment opportunities by
prioritizing local labor.
